# POLARIS — Locked Scope (Carney demo)

**Status:** LOCKED 2026-05-15. **Issue:** I-rdy-001 (#497), Phase 0A of the
Carney demo execution plan (`state/carney_demo_execution_plan_2026_05_15.md`).

**Purpose:** the single anti-drift source of truth for the Carney demo. The
LLM, the architecture, and the feature scope are frozen here. If a Codex
consult, an advisor, an external doc, or Claude's own reasoning ever proposes
something that contradicts §1, this document wins. Changes to §1 require an
explicit operator-signed reconciliation commit — not a silent edit.

**Pinning:** §1 (constraints) is LOCKED at this commit; its commit SHA is the
pin. §3 feature statuses are **PROVISIONAL** pending Phase 1 verification
(I-rdy-002, #498); the full document is re-pinned in Phase 0B (I-rdy-003,
#499) once statuses are verified.

---

## §1. Locked constraints — frozen, do not reopen

### §1.1 LLM
- **Generator: DeepSeek V4 Pro** — 1.6-trillion-parameter MoE (49B active/token), ships FP4 (experts) + FP8 (rest). Operator-locked, stated 6+ times.
- **Evaluator: Gemma 4 31B.**
- **Two-family invariant:** generator (DeepSeek lineage) and evaluator (Google/Gemma lineage) are different training lineages. `openrouter_client.check_family_segregation` enforces this at construction.
- **Concurrency: 1 concurrent session.** Traffic is operator-limited. This does not shrink the GPU requirement (the 1.6T model needs ~880 GB VRAM regardless of concurrency); it only bounds KV-cache headroom.
- Supersedes the prior `docs/hardware_decision.md` "Path C / V4 Flash" — that was overridden by operator directive to V4 Pro.

### §1.2 Hardware / sovereignty
- **Canadian sovereign GPU only.** V4 Pro requires 8× H200 or 12-16× H100 (Hopper-class — V100/A100 lack FP4/FP8 and cannot run it).
- **Not** OVH Canada — its Canadian datacentre (Beauharnois, Québec) has only old V100/V100S GPUs, which lack FP4/FP8 and cannot run V4 Pro; OVH's Hopper-class H100/H200/A100 are France-only (verified via the OVH API; see `state/canada_gpu_research_2026_05_15.md`). **Not** France. **Not** any US company.
- Canadian sovereign GPU candidates: Vexxhost (Montréal), ISAIC (Edmonton) — both pending physical-location confirmation.
- Sovereignty threat model is NARROW: no runtime US LLM vendor calls + no report data in US jurisdiction. Model lineage, build toolchain, and DNS registrar are out of scope of the threat model.
- The orchestrator (CPU box) stays on OVH BHS5 — that is Canadian and acceptable; only the LLM inference must move to a Canadian GPU.

### §1.3 Architecture
- **The deployed v6 stack** is the architecture: `redis + api (FastAPI v6.2.0) + worker (Dramatiq) + webui (Next.js 16)` via `docker-compose.v6.yml`. **No rewrites.**
- Pipeline B (legacy LangGraph) and Pipeline C (frozen CLI) are **out of demo scope.**
- Frontend stack: Next.js 16 + React 19 + shadcn/ui + Tailwind 4.

### §1.4 Naming
- The legacy name **"BPEI" is banned** in all demo-facing code, docs, and config. Renamed to `ambiguity_detector`. Rename tracked by #434 (I-naming-001) + readiness Phase 2.

### §1.5 Demo
- Single venue, PM Mark Carney's office, flexible date in June 2026.
- Scope = the Carney plan (`docs/carney_delivery_plan_v6_2.md`). **No features beyond it.**

---

# Canada GPU — deep research for V4 Pro sovereign deployment

**Date:** 2026-05-15. **Question:** can V4 Pro run on OVH old GPU / where can we get Canadian GPU.
**Status:** research complete. One hard finding the operator must decide on.

---

## 1. What V4 Pro actually is (verified)

- **DeepSeek V4 Pro = 1.6 trillion total parameters**, Mixture-of-Experts, 49 billion active per token.
- Ships in **mixed precision**: MoE expert weights are **FP4**; the rest are **FP8**.
- 1M token context. Released 2026. MIT license. Weights on HuggingFace (`deepseek-ai/DeepSeek-V4-Pro`).
- **Memory to hold the model ≈ 880 GB** at its native FP4/FP8 precision.
- Source consensus: HuggingFace model card, Together AI, SiliconFlow, Modular, framia.pro all say 1.6T/49B. (One page — WaveSpeed — said "671B"; that is stale V3 data mislabelled. Even if it were 671B, the V100 conclusion below is unchanged.)

## 2. The hardware rule that decides everything

- FP4 and FP8 are **new number formats**. Only **NVIDIA Hopper (H100, H200)** and **Blackwell (B200)** GPUs can run them in hardware.
- **V100 (2017, "Volta")**: NO FP8, NO FP4, NO BF16, NO FlashAttention. It only does FP16/FP32.
- **A100 (2020, "Ampere")**: NO FP8, NO FP4. It does BF16/FP16/INT8.
- To run V4 Pro on a V100 or A100 you must **up-convert** the weights — FP4→FP16 makes them **4× bigger**, FP8→FP16 makes them **2× bigger**.

## 3. OVH old GPU (V100) — how many cards, honest answer

- OVH's Beauharnois (Quebec) datacentre has only **V100 / V100S** GPUs. No H100/H200/A100 in Canada (verified earlier via OVH API + 3rd-party).
- V4 Pro on V100 means up-converting everything to FP16: **880 GB native → roughly 3,200 GB in FP16**.
- V100 = 32 GB each. 3,200 GB ÷ 32 GB = **~100 V100 cards** just to hold the model. ~110-120 with working memory.
- **But the card count is the wrong question. It does not work at any count, because:**
  1. V100 has no FP8/FP4 hardware → forced 4× memory blow-up (above).
  2. V100 has no FlashAttention → V4 Pro's 1M-token attention runs catastrophically slow.
  3. OVH sells V100 only as small VMs (1, 2, or 4 GPUs each). 100 V100s = ~30 separate VMs with **no fast GPU-to-GPU link between them**. You cannot split a 1.6T model across 30 disconnected VMs.
  4. Even if somehow assembled, output speed would be a fraction of one word per second — a single report would take many hours.
- **Verdict: OVH old GPU is not "too expensive" — it is the wrong tool. A 2017 GPU cannot run a 2026 1.6-trillion-parameter FP4 model. Dead path. Do not pursue.**

## 4. What V4 Pro actually needs

- **8× H200** (141 GB each = 1,128 GB total) — the clean native config. Holds the model + room for context.
- OR **12-16× H100** (80 GB each) — works, tighter.
- **1 concurrent session does NOT reduce this.** The 880 GB is the model's own weight. Limiting traffic only saves a little context memory; it does not shrink the model. You still need 8× H200 (or 12-16× H100) minimum just to load V4 Pro.
- Cost, if Canadian H100 is found: at ISAIC's claimed $2.50 CAD/hr per H100, 16× H100 = $40/hr. A 5-day demo at ~15 hours uptime ≈ **$600 CAD**. Cost is NOT the blocker. **Availability is.**

## 5. ISAIC (Edmonton) — the honest picture

- ISAIC is a genuine Canadian non-profit, Edmonton, tied to University of Alberta. Sovereignty positioning real ("your data never leaves Canada").
- **Confirmed on their own site:** A100 80GB at **$1.63 CAD/hr**. A100 (Ampere) **cannot natively run V4 Pro** (no FP8/FP4) — would need ~40× A100, impractical.
- **They claim H100 at $2.50 CAD/hr.** H100 (Hopper) WOULD run V4 Pro.
- **CRITICAL CAVEAT (3rd-party April 2026 review):** ISAIC's listed H100s "may not be in Canada but rather in Gravelines, France." If ISAIC's H100 is physically in France, it FAILS the sovereignty requirement — same problem as OVH.
- **Action needed: contact ISAIC and ask one specific question — "Are your H100 GPUs physically in Edmonton, Alberta, or in France? We need Canadian-located H100."**

## 6. Vexxhost (Montréal) — the honest picture

- Genuine Canadian company, Montréal HQ, sovereign-cloud positioning.
- Public self-serve pricing page shows only old K80 GPUs. H100 is NOT self-serve — needs a sales conversation.
- We have portal credentials but the OpenStack API needs an Application Credential (operator must create it from the portal).
- **Action needed: contact Vexxhost sales — "Do you have 8× H200 or 16× H100, physically in Montréal, available June 5-9 2026?"**

## 7. Vast.ai — not a sovereign option

- Vast.ai owns no datacentres. It is a marketplace; hosts are independent individuals/companies.
- It has a country filter, so Canadian-located rigs may appear.
- But you cannot attest sovereignty: you do not know who legally owns the rig or what jurisdiction's laws apply. For a Prime-Minister-of-Canada demo this is not defensible.
- Earlier POLARIS planning correctly treated Vast as a US dev cluster only, never the sovereign path.

## 8. US companies with Canadian regions (listed for honesty — all FAIL sovereignty)

- DigitalOcean Toronto: has H100 (~$2.99/hr USD) but US company; reported sold out April 2026.
- AWS ca-central-1: H100/A100 on the sheet, US company, zero default quota, approval routes through US.
- Google Cloud Montreal/Toronto: only old or inference-only GPU, US company.
- Azure Canada Central: some GPU, US company.

## 9. The honest collision the operator must resolve

Two locked constraints **may not have a clean intersection**:
- **V4 Pro (locked)** → needs Hopper H100/H200.
- **Canadian sovereign (locked)** → Canada's H100/H200 supply is genuinely thin.

The only two candidate Canadian H100 sources both need verification:
- **ISAIC H100** — must confirm it is physically in Edmonton, not France.
- **Vexxhost H100/H200** — must confirm availability + Montréal location via sales.

If BOTH turn out unavailable in Canada, that is a genuine halt-and-decide for the operator (not Claude): accept non-Canadian H100, or change model, or move the date. Claude does not choose — Claude reports.

## 10. Recommended immediate actions (operator + Claude split)

- **Operator:** contact ISAIC (info@isaic.ca) — ask the §5 question. Contact Vexxhost sales — ask the §6 question.
- **Claude:** can draft both emails now. Can also pre-stage everything provider-agnostic (vLLM config for V4 Pro, generator/evaluator URL separation) so the day a Canadian H100 is confirmed, deployment is fast.
- **Do NOT pursue OVH V100.** It is a dead path — §3.

state/canada_gpu_research_2026_05_15.md:19:- **V100 (2017, "Volta")**: NO FP8, NO FP4, NO BF16, NO FlashAttention. It only does FP16/FP32.
state/canada_gpu_research_2026_05_15.md:21:- To run V4 Pro on a V100 or A100 you must **up-convert** the weights — FP4→FP16 makes them **4× bigger**, FP8→FP16 makes them **2× bigger**.
state/canada_gpu_research_2026_05_15.md:23:## 3. OVH old GPU (V100) — how many cards, honest answer
state/canada_gpu_research_2026_05_15.md:25:- OVH's Beauharnois (Quebec) datacentre has only **V100 / V100S** GPUs. No H100/H200/A100 in Canada (verified earlier via OVH API + 3rd-party).
state/canada_gpu_research_2026_05_15.md:26:- V4 Pro on V100 means up-converting everything to FP16: **880 GB native → roughly 3,200 GB in FP16**.
state/canada_gpu_research_2026_05_15.md:27:- V100 = 32 GB each. 3,200 GB ÷ 32 GB = **~100 V100 cards** just to hold the model. ~110-120 with working memory.
state/canada_gpu_research_2026_05_15.md:29:  1. V100 has no FP8/FP4 hardware → forced 4× memory blow-up (above).
state/canada_gpu_research_2026_05_15.md:30:  2. V100 has no FlashAttention → V4 Pro's 1M-token attention runs catastrophically slow.
state/canada_gpu_research_2026_05_15.md:31:  3. OVH sells V100 only as small VMs (1, 2, or 4 GPUs each). 100 V100s = ~30 separate VMs with **no fast GPU-to-GPU link between them**. You cannot split a 1.6T model across 30 disconnected VMs.
state/canada_gpu_research_2026_05_15.md:87:- **Do NOT pursue OVH V100.** It is a dead path — §3.

   1: # POLARIS Carney demo — readiness gap register
   2: 
   3: **Date:** 2026-05-15. **Source:** Claude grounded inventory + Codex independent
   4: gap-analysis consult (`.codex/carney-readiness-gap-analysis/`).
   5: **Codex verdict:** REQUEST_CHANGES — the readiness picture is missing material parts.
   6: 
   7: ---
   8: 
   9: ## The core finding (deeper than "test harness pages")
  10: 
  11: POLARIS's features were each built as **substrates + test-harness pages + golden
  12: fixtures**, and each GitHub issue was completed *as scoped* and Codex-reviewed.
  13: BUT the **rich product surfaces do not consume live run output** — the inspector,
  14: charts, follow-up, compare, bundle paths are **fixture-bound** (they display
  15: golden test fixtures, not the artifacts a real run produces).
  16: 
  17: Consequence: even with a working LLM, a real run will produce a run — but
  18: click-through inspection, chart provenance, follow-up, compare, pin replay,
  19: memory, and bundle export **from that same run** are not wired. This is the
  20: `bpei_phantom_completion` pattern: issue-level "done" ≠ product-level "done."
  21: 
  22: So the GPU-wait window is NOT "testing time." It is **integration time** — and
  23: that work mostly does not need the GPU.
  24: 
  25: ---
  26: 
  27: ## Per-feature honest status (Codex + Claude)
  28: 
  29: | Feature | Substrate | Product-integrated? |
  30: |---|---|---|
  31: | F1 scope/templates | landing UI exists | template cards don't match canonical templates |
  32: | F2 ambiguity detector | backend + modal exist | not consistently wired into main run flow |
  33: | F3 upload/grounding | upload UI + API exist | only text/markdown parse fully; async runs don't consume uploaded docs |
  34: | F4 live audit SSE | Redis SSE substrate works | run page shows raw event cards, not frontier-grade |

.codex\carney-execution-plan-review\codex_verdict.txt:1521:19795:docs\transparency.md:72:The rationale for accepting the exception: POLARIS's sovereignty constraint protects the **LLM inference path and the generated report data** — those run on Canadian/non-US infrastructure (Vexxhost orchestrator + OVH H200 inference). .codex\carney-hardware-spec\consult_brief_cost.md:13:- **Generator model size is the entire cost problem.** DeepSeek V4 Pro / V4 Flash are V3-lineage MoE (~600B+ params). POLARIS's own `docs/hardware_decision.md` sizes them at **8× H200** (V4 Flash / V4 Pro-reduced) or **16× H200** (V4 Pro full). A 600B-class model genuinely needs that.
.codex\carney-hardware-spec\consult_brief_cost.md:14:- **OVH Canada GPU pricing (Claude verified via live API today, CAD):** `h200-1920` (8× H200 node) = **$67.20/hr** / $49,056/mo. `h100-380` (1× H100 80GB) = **$4.05/hr** / $2,810/mo. `h100-760` (2× H100) = $8.10/hr. OVH Public Cloud GPU is hourly-billable; no sub-hour granularity; no serverless-GPU product.
.codex\carney-hardware-spec\consult_brief_cost.md:16:- **Cold-start tax:** spinning a 600B model on a fresh 8× H200 instance = instance boot + loading ~600 GB of weights into HBM ≈ 10–20+ min. A 70B-class model on 1× H100 ≈ 5–6 min cold start. **Per-request** spin-up is therefore NOT smooth for either; **session-scoped** spin-up (warm for a 2–4 hr rehearsal/demo block, killed after) is the realistic version of the operator's instinct.
.codex\carney-hardware-spec\consult_brief_cost.md:22:- **Drop $300 cap** → 8× H200 session-scoped, ~$670 for the whole demo. Sovereign, smooth, full V4-class. Over budget ~2×.
.codex\carney-hardware-spec\consult_brief_cost.md:23:- **Drop 600B model** → strong **70B-class open-weight sovereign model** on 1× H100 (`h100-380`, $4.05/hr). Session-scoped ~12 hrs = **~$50**. Evaluator Gemma 4 31B co-hosts or shares. Fully sovereign, smooth, ~$300/mo with huge margin. **Cost: the generator is 70B not 600B** — but POLARIS's faithfulness edge is the `strict_verify` provenance-token architecture, not raw model size; a 70B generator may lose little on POLARIS's actual task (cited synthesis from retrieved evidence).
.codex\carney-hardware-spec\consult_brief_cost.md:25:- **Drop smooth** → per-request spin-up of 8× H200 with a 10–20 min cold-start tax per query. Not a Carney-grade experience.

state\codex_consultations\plan_v7_codex_response.md:1486:outputs\polaris_graph\GEMINI_E2E_20260316_092353_report.md:1:# Comparative Analysis of Adhesion Testing Methods for Polymer Coatings: Methodologies, Performance, and Regulatory Compliance ## Abstract This research report presents a comparative analysis of adhesion testing methods for polymer coatings, specifically evaluating pull-off, cross-cut, and peel tests across epoxy, urethane, and silicone materials. Spanning 15 sections and comprising 16,701 words, the study examines standardized test method definitions within ASTM and ISO frameworks alongside material suitability limits and thickness constraints. The methodology involves a rigorous review of ASTM D4541 protocols for pull-off testing and ASTM D903 and ISO11339 compliance for peel test variations, including 90-degree and T-peel configurations. Furthermore, the analysis addresses knife and scrape test methods for hard coatings, surface contaminants, and adhesion performance scoring scales. Key findings highlight the impact of environmental temperature and immersion effects on adhesion, alongside theoretical adhesion modeling and cohesive failure analysis. The report classifies failure modes and mechanism units while assessing urethane adhesive capabilities and bond strength applications. Administrative testing documentation and warranty requirements are also scrutinized to ensure regulatory compliance. Drawing upon 84 total unique sources cited and incorporating 218 total citations in report, this work establishes critical guidelines for selecting appropriate testing methodologies. The implications suggest that strict adherence to standardized protocols enhances reliability in predicting coating performance under varying environmental conditions, thereby informing industry best practices for quality assurance and warranty validation in polymer coating applications. ## Standardized Test Method Definitions and ASTM ISO Frameworks Adhesion testing frameworks for polymer coatings are governed by a complex array of ASTM and ISO standards that define methodological rigor and compliance thresholds [1]. Additionally, these standardized protocols establish the baseline for comparing pull-off, cross-cut, and peel tests across epoxy, urethane, and silicone materials [2]. Furthermore, the regulatory landscape distinguishes between quantitative tensile measurements and qualitative visual assessments, each serving specific diagnostic purposes within the coating lifecycle [3]. Pull-off adhesion testing applies tensile force perpendicular to the substrate to measure coating resistance to separation [1]. This method is utilized to gage adhesive strength of the coating once applied and cured as per manufacturer's guidelines [2]. Moreover, the pull-off adhesion tester measures adhesion of coatings to metal, wood, concrete, and rigid substrates, providing versatility across material classes [4]. Pull-off test data provides quantitative indication of bond tensile strength [5]. In addition, industrial-grade adhesives typically have tensile strength ranging from 10 MPa to over 60 MPa depending on formulation [6]. Hydroxyapatite coatings showed repeatable adhesion strength of 3.4 ± 1.0 MPa across both ASTM test methods [7]. ASTM C633-01 test uses the reported value diameter cylindrical blocks with 38 mm length for adhesion testing. Moreover, ASTM D4541 and BS EN ISO 4624 describe pull-off adhesion testing standards for protective coatings [8]. The pull-off test is standardized by ISO 4624:2023 and is widely used to quantify adhesion [9]. ASTM D7234 defines the standard test method for pull-off adhesion strength on concrete using portable testers [10]. Pull-off testing follows ISO 4624, ASTM D4541, and D7234 standards for evaluating coating peel strength [11]. Valid pull-off test requires coating to cover at least 50% of stamp surface after detachment [11]. In addition, the Cross-Cut Test provides qualitative evaluation while the Pull-Off Method provides quantitative evaluation of coating adhesion [3]. This distinction is critical when selecting methods for epoxy, urethane, or silicone systems, as quantitative data may be required for structural warranty validation. Additionally, cross-Cut Test classifies adhesion results into six levels according to the degree of peeling after tape removal [3]. The procedure involves making lattice-pattern cuts through the coating to the substrate, applying adhesive tape, and peeling in one motion [3]. ASTM D3359 outlines standardized test methods for evaluating adhesion of coatings to metallic substrates using pressure-sensitive tape [12]. This standard is widely recognized for evaluating coating adhesion on metal substrates using the tape test method [13]. ISO 2409 specifies the cross-cut test method for evaluating adhesion of coatings on sheet metal parts [13]. ASTM D3359 defines adhesion as molecular attraction and mechanical bonds between coating and substrate [14]. ASTM D3359 procedures assess the adhesion of coating films specifically on metallic substrates [15]. The tape test involves applying and removing pressure-sensitive tape over cuts made in the film [15]. The tape test is qualitative and cannot precisely assess quantitatively the actual strength of the adhesion [16]. Cross-Cut Test uses pressure-sensitive tape spread over cuts and rapidly pulled off [17]. Adhesion is rated by inspecting the cut area for coating removal relative to classifications 0B through 5B [16]. Cross-cut adhesion test evaluation ranges from 0B representing complete detachment to 5B representing no detachment [18]. ASTM D3359 Standard Test Method for Measuring Adhesion by Tape Test was codified in 1974 [19]. ASTM D3359 is a standard test method for measuring adhesion by tape test [20]. Crosshatch adhesion testing follows ASTM D3359 standard with grid cuts through coating [21]. ASTM D3359 is the standard method for rating adhesion by tape test [22]. Peel testing represents a third category, measuring the force required to strip a flexible coating from a substrate [23]. Peel tests measure adhesive strength by pulling one material away from another at a specific angle and speed [24]. This method is suitable for designs where bonded edges experience prying or bending [25]. Peel and adhesion strength testing measures bond strength when force attempts separation [25]. Comprehensive adhesive suitability requires comparing peel adhesion, tensile strength, and shear resistance [26]. The peel test is a method used in materials science to measure the bond strength between two materials [27]. Peel strength is usually reported in units of force per width such as N/mm or lb/in [27]. Peel testing methods include T-peel and 90-degree peel tests standardized under ASTM D1876 and D6862 [28]. Standard testing procedures evaluate peel force under constant temperature 23±1°C and 50±5% RH [29]. Peel test values represent an average between 5 and the reported value of peel for each of two tests. T-peel testing was performed according to BS 5350-C12:1994/ISO 11339:1993 standard on aluminium alloy [30]. Chinese national standard GB/T 2790-1995 specifies the 180° peel strength test method steps and requirements [31]. ISO 8510-2 covers 180-degree peel test for flexible-bonded-to-rigid test specimen assembly [24]. The T shaped peel test ASTM D1876 is most widely used for thin-gauge metal adherends [26]. Table 1 summarizes the comparative frameworks for these primary adhesion testing methods, highlighting standard designations and operational limits. | Test Method | Primary Standard (ASTM/ISO) | Substrate Suitability | Measurement Type | Thickness Limit | |:--- |:--- |:--- |:--- |:--- | | Pull-Off | ASTM D4541, ISO 4624 | Metal, Concrete, Rigid | Quantitative (MPa) | N/A [11] | | Cross-Cut | ASTM D3359, ISO 2409 | Metal, Plastic, Rigid | Qualitative (0B-5B) | 250 µm [32] | | Peel | ASTM D1876, ISO 11339 | Flexible-to-Rigid | Quantitative (N/mm) | N/A [28] | | Knife/Scrape | ASTM D6677 | Hard Coatings | Qualitative/Force | N/A [22] | Thickness constraints significantly influence method selection for polymer coatings. The Cross-Cut Test is limited by coating thickness with a maximum of 250 micrometers [32]. Cross-cut test is applicable for layer thicknesses up to about 250 µm [33]. This limitation renders the cross-cut method unsuitable for thick urethane or epoxy linings often exceeding this threshold. The test method covers determination of practical adhesion strength of hard ceramic coatings on metal and ceramic substrates [34]. Test method is commonly used for coating thicknesses in the range of 0.1 to 30 micrometers [34]. Adhesion pull-off strength testing determines the overall quality and integrity of an installed coating system [35]. Cross hatch tape testing is intended for laboratory use on coatings under 5 mils thickness [22]. Scrape test adhesion is measured in kilograms of force required for removal [36]. ASTM D6677 is the standard method for evaluating adhesion by knife [22]. Cross-cut test is especially suitable for thin layers on rigid substrates like metal or plastic [33]. The crosscut adhesion test is a standard ASTM or ISO procedure for dry coatings [37]. Conflicting evidence exists regarding the fundamental capabilities of these tests and specific procedural dimensions. One source states the pull-off adhesion test measures the strength of the bond between a coating and the surface [4]. However, conflicting evidence indicates the test method does not measure fundamental adhesion strength of the bond between coating and substrate [34]. This discrepancy suggests a distinction between practical field adhesion and theoretical interfacial strength. Regarding cut patterns, evidence states cross-cut test uses a tool to cut a right-angle lattice pattern through the coating to the substrate [1]. Other evidence indicates X-cut tape test uses a sharp tool to make an X-shaped cut through the coating to substrate [4]. ASTM D3359 accommodates both via Method A (X-Cut) and Method B (Cross-Cut Tape Test), with Method B preferred for laboratory or controlled environments [12]. Test Method A (X-Cut Tape Test) is designed primarily for field use with X-shaped cut in coating. Cross-Hatch Test is typically used on witness samples of the same substrate material [38]. Procedural variations also appear in peel test specimen dimensions. Evidence indicates DOWSIL ALLGUARD coating adhesion test uses cheesecloth strip 1 inch by 12 inches embedded in wet coating [39]. In conflict, another source states peel test uses 1 inch wide by 6 inch long fabric strip embedded in coating base layer [40]. These variations likely reflect specific manufacturer protocols versus general standardized methods. ISO 4624 is referenced as a global standard for pull-off adhesion testing compliance [5]. Traditional adhesion tests like ISO/JIS cross-hatch and ASTM tape tests only evaluate final adhesion strength without explaining adhesion mechanisms [41]. Surface free energy and adhesion parameter measurements are proposed as complementary methods to traditional cross-cut tape tests [41]. Adhesion testing mechanics are complicated when polymer adhesive and metal substrate have different elastic moduli [42]. This complexity is particularly relevant for silicone and urethane coatings which exhibit different elastic properties compared to epoxy systems. Peel and pull-off data should not be compared since testing mechanisms differ [14]. Four common methods exist for testing adhesion of protective coatings and linings per ASTM standards [14]. Common adhesion measuring techniques are performed using a knife or a pull-off adhesion tester. Specific material considerations emerge within the evidence regarding epoxy and silicone systems. Results showed good agreement between hot water-adhesion tests and TxDOT peel tests [43]. This suggests environmental conditioning impacts epoxy coating performance assessment. The majority of bond systems underwent tensile adhesion strength testing using a button-to-plate configuration [44]. Silicone contamination impacts bond performance, necessitating rigorous testing protocols [44]. Video How to Perform an Adhesion Test Patch provides more information on roof coating adhesion tests [45]. This indicates field verification is critical for urethane and silicone roof applications. Adhesion test must be conducted prior to estimating coating restoration project per Inland adhesion testing procedures [46]. Adhesive performance evaluation after testing includes checking bonding strength, color stability, surface integrity, and chemical stability [47]. Tensile or shear tests measure strength between bonded materials for adhesives [48]. The study objective was to identify an optimal adhesive for the pull-off test providing higher adhesion [9]. Pull-off method provides values for peel stress allowing quick determination and comparison of coating adhesion [11]. The proposed adhesion testing method uses direct pull-off strength measurements at various corrosion stages [49]. Field adhesion test recommended when laboratory test cannot be performed on-site [50]. Field adhesion test also called hand pull test verifies proper sealant installation. There is no universally accepted test for conformal coating adhesion [38]. SAE J400 standard outlines specialized coating adhesion test method using Instron testing machine [13]. Latter two ASTM methods evaluate tensile or pull-off strength of coating [14]. The test method was originally developed for ductile coatings applied to metal substrates [16]. Site adhesion testing must be verified according to criteria on page 35 to confirm proper surface preparation [51]. Nitto Denko employs adhesive pull strength evaluation using JIS Z0237 and ASTM standards [52]. Mechanical analysis services include testing for adhesive strength, tensile strength, yield stress. All structural applications must be tested by the Dow test lab for surface preparation recommendations. A similar testing program is needed for concrete coatings to enable laboratory verification. Adhesive testing products include epoxy glue, polyurethane glue, and organic silicone among many others [47]. Tensile bulk tests were performed using long dogbone specimens to characterize adhesives at room temperature [53]. Higher yield strength values obtained in compression test than in tensile test for all adhesives. Study of size dependence of bulk specimen was carried out to evaluate scale factors in adhesive testing. NPL and partners developed a tape peel test applicable to PCB or component surfaces [54]. ATS offers ASTM paint adhesion testing to determine adhesive performance according to industry standards. Peel tests involve pulling flexible material from a surface at defined angles [48]. **Key Findings** * Pull-off methods (ASTM D4541, ISO 4624) provide quantitative tensile strength data suitable for thick epoxy and urethane systems, whereas cross-cut tests (ASTM D3359) offer qualitative ratings limited to coatings under 250 µm [32]. * Conflicting evidence exists regarding whether pull-off tests measure fundamental adhesion strength or practical bond integrity, indicating a need for method selection based on specific performance criteria [4][34]. * Peel testing standards (ASTM D1876, ISO 11339) quantify force per width (N/mm) for flexible-to-rigid bonds, essential for silicone and urethane membrane evaluations [28][27]. * ASTM D3359 encompasses both X-cut and lattice patterns, with the lattice method preferred for laboratory precision and the X-cut for field applications [1]. * Environmental conditions and substrate elastic moduli significantly influence test outcomes, requiring controlled temperature and humidity during peel and tensile assessments [42][29]. ! Standard Number for Tape Test | 3359 | N/A | | Standard Original Adoption Year | 2005 | 2005 | | Standard Reapproval Year | 2010 | 2010 | *Table 1: ASTM Standard Identifiers and Years* ## Material Suitability Limits and Thickness Constraints Selection of appropriate testing protocols depends critically on material suitability limits and thickness constraints within polymer coating systems. Adhesion strength of coating to substrate defines overall coating integrity and performance [55]. Significantly, the applicability of specific adhesion testing methods varies significantly based on the physical properties of the coating material and the thickness of the applied layer. Crosshatch testing is most suitable for thin coatings on rigid substrates [21]. Thickness constraints serve as a primary determinant for method selection across epoxy, urethane, and silicone materials. The evidence indicates distinct operational ranges for common testing methodologies. Top coat thickness ranged from 28 to 638 micrometers in the duplex coating system [56]. Cured adhesive thicknesses were measured at 2.0 plus or minus 0.1 mils for testing [57]. | Test Method / Material Context | Thickness Constraint / Measurement | Unit | Source ID |. | Cross-Cut Test (General) | Maximum 250 | micrometers | |. | Cross-Cut Test (Laboratory) | Under 5 | mils | |. | ASTM C1624 (Hard Ceramic) | 0.1 to 30 | micrometers | |. | Duplex Coating System | 28 to 638 | micrometers | [56] |. | Cured Adhesive Specimen | 2.0 plus or minus 0.1 | mils | [57] |. The data demonstrates that cross-cut methods possess strict upper limits compared to pull-off configurations. For coatings up to 2.0 mils thickness, cuts are spaced the reported value apart with eleven cuts. Exceeding these thresholds compromises the lattice pattern integrity required for evaluation. Force measured in MPa or psi reveals the coating's tensile pull-off strength indicating adhesion [36]. Most concrete coating adhesion pull-off strength testing exhibits substrate failure in the 400-500 psi range [35]. The adhesion pull-off strength of the polyaspartic published in its data sheet is 900 psi [35]. Material suitability extends beyond thickness to include chemical composition and mechanical stiffness. Polyurethane coatings can possess higher stiffness than the adhesive used to fix the testing dolly in place [58]. Polyurethane coatings offer super flexibility, abrasion resistance, and UV stability for outdoor applications [5]. Increasing hard segment content in waterborne polyurethane formulation usually increases peel strength [31]. Epoxy coatings must conform to MPI ASM No. 116 with polyamide-cured epoxy-resin material for finish [59]. Epoxy bonded to silicone duplex coating pull-off force thickness dependence agreed with modified Kendall model for elastomeric coatings [56]. Category 4 silicone-resin coatings must contain minimum the reported value silicone resin and maximum 49 percent. Methodological distinctions between qualitative and quantitative evaluation impact data reliability for these materials. Peel strength is calculated using force applied during peeling process divided by film width. This limitation suggests that while pull-off testing measures the strength of the bond between a coating and the surface, it may not isolate interfacial chemistry. Conflicting evidence exists regarding specific procedural dimensions and measurement capabilities. This discrepancy indicates variability in specimen preparation standards across different manufacturers and material types. Surface prep chemistry shapes coating outcomes ranging from adhesion to corrosion resistance and durability [60]. Failure to verify and document adhesion results may result in adhesion loss not covered by Dow Warranty [51]. Tensile modulus was measured for three polyurethane coatings, an epoxy adhesive, and a cyanoacrylate adhesive [58]. Significantly, differences in Young's modulus between test methods and manufacturer data reached up to 70% for methods [61]. Adhesive bonds can be stronger than the materials being bonded together in some automotive applications. Tungsten carbide coating adhesion exceeded epoxy adhesive strength, limiting measurement to 95 MPa maximum [7]. Cohesive strength decreases as coating undergoes weathering and pull-off strength monitors breakdown [62]. The electronics industry has demanding applications with specific adhesive performance requirements. Adhesives and potting compounds are used for die attach, glob top encapsulation, underfill, edge bonding [63]. Market expectations for removable adhesives specify 1.75 pli peel strength after 3 weeks at 70C [57]. Key peel test metrics include initial peak force, average force across entire seal, and peel strength [24]. 3M 681 tape was found to most closely reflect desired properties and performance for adhesion testing [19]. Cross-cut testing measures coating resistance to separation by cutting a right-angle lattice pattern to the substrate [23]. Acceptable adhesion shows cohesive tear within sealant or elongation beyond movement capability [50]. Mode of failure and pull-off pressure confirm proper surface preparation and coating application [2]. Failure mode for BTSE/GPS coated surface is cohesive failure when specimen is dry with fracture surface [64]. Pull-off strength important parameter when selecting polymer coating systems [62]. Coating application quality varies by coater and impacts adhesion [43]. Two coats necessary to achieve 10-mil minimum dry film thickness for coating [39]. Technology assessment analyzed 94 individual coatings and 44 coating systems for application and durability [65]. First-cost comparisons rank acrylic least expensive, silicone close behind, polyurethane higher [66]. Polyurethane coatings provide high abrasion resistance, good flexibility, chemical resistance, and UV stability [59]. * Cross-cut testing is constrained to thinner coatings, with maximum limits identified at 250 micrometers or under 5 mils, whereas pull-off methods accommodate thicker duplex systems ranging up to 638 micrometers [56]. * Polyurethane and silicone materials require specific consideration of stiffness and resin content, as polyurethane coatings can exceed the stiffness of testing adhesives and silicone-resin coatings mandate minimum 22 percent resin composition [58]. * Method selection dictates data type, with cross-cut tests providing qualitative evaluation via lattice patterns and pull-off tests delivering quantitative tensile strength measurements in MPa or psi [36]. * Procedural conflicts exist in specimen preparation, evidenced by divergent peel test strip dimensions of 1x6 inches versus 1x12 inches depending on the specific coating manufacturer standards [40][39]. Specifically, *Figure 2: Bar chart displaying document engagement metrics such as views and ratings. | Published Year | 2019 | year |. *Table 1: Key Timeline Events for ASTM C1624*. ## Pull-Off Testing Mechanical Application and Procedures Pull-off testing represents a primary quantitative methodology for evaluating bond strength, distinct from qualitative assessments found in cross-cut or peel configurations. This perpendicular application differentiates pull-off mechanics from peel tests, which measure the force required to strip a flexible coating from a substrate at specific angles [23]. In particular, for polymer coatings such as epoxy, urethane, and silicone, the choice of mechanical application directly influences the interpretability of adhesion strength regarding regulatory compliance and field performance. The core mechanism utilizes a fixture, often referred to as a dolly, which is adhesively bonded to the cured coating surface [36]. Once bonded, the tester exerts a pulling force until failure occurs, providing a quantitative indication of bond tensile strength. This mechanical setup allows for the direct determination of adhesion strength at the metallisation or polymer interface from the substrate [67]. The mechanical distinction is critical for the research question comparing methods across materials; pull-off tests are typical for industrial coatings including polymer coatings and epoxy resins, whereas cross-cut tests are especially suitable for thin layers on rigid substrates like metal or plastic. Standardization governs the mechanical procedures to ensure comparability across epoxy, urethane, and silicone materials. The evolution of these standards reflects the industry's shift toward quantitative evaluation methods. The following table outlines key standards and their establishment or revision dates relevant to mechanical adhesion testing procedures. | Standard Designation | Method Type | Establishment/Revision Year | Evaluation Type |. | ASTM D3359 | Tape Test | 1974 [19] | Qualitative |. | JIS K 5600-5-6 | Cross-Cut Test | 1999 | Qualitative |. | JIS K 5600-5-7 | Pull-Off Method | 2014 | Quantitative |. | ISO 4624 | Pull-Off Adhesion | 2023 [9] | Quantitative [9] |. The temporal progression in the table indicates a standardization trend toward quantitative tensile testing methods like ISO 4624 and JIS K 5600-5-7. However, conflicting evidence exists regarding the specific scope and dates of Japanese Industrial Standards. One source specifies JIS K 5600-5-7: 2014 for the Pull-Off Method as a coating tensile adhesion test for quantitative evaluation. Another source indicates JIS K 5600-5-6: 1999 specifies the Cross-Cut Test method for coating adhesion evaluation under Japanese Industrial Standards. While the conflict detector flags this as a contradiction, the evidence suggests these represent different parts of the same standard framework addressing different mechanical methods rather than mutually exclusive facts. The 1999 date applies to cross-cut mechanics, while the 2014 date applies to pull-off mechanics, highlighting the divergent procedural requirements within the same regulatory body. Similarly, distinctions in ASTM standards clarify the mechanical application differences. These standards support different mechanical actions; the tape test relies on adhesive removal force, while the knife test evaluates resistance to scraping. Quantitative outcomes from pull-off mechanics offer specific metrics for epoxy and silicone materials. Specific material data shows a button pull-off test measured exterior paint average bond strength at 7.27 MPa (1050 psi) [68]. These numerical values underscore the quantitative advantage of pull-off mechanics over the 0B to 5B classification range found in cross-cut adhesion test evaluation [18]. For silicone rubber and epoxy resin interfaces, pull-off tests were applied to estimate adhesion effect, demonstrating utility across these specific polymer types [69]. Mechanical application procedures also dictate sample validity. This requirement ensures that the measured force reflects coating adhesion rather than adhesive failure between the dolly and the coating. This mechanical difference implies that peel tests may better simulate edge failures in flexible silicone or urethane coatings, while pull-off tests better simulate normal stress failures in rigid epoxy systems. Environmental and procedural factors further influence mechanical test outcomes. These factors are critical when comparing methods across materials, as urethane and silicone adhesives may cure differently under varying conditions. In particular, relying solely on one mechanical method, such as the cross-cut test which uses pressure-sensitive tape spread over cuts and rapidly pulled off, may not capture the full performance profile of complex polymer systems [17]. Field application mechanics differ from laboratory settings. A simple qualitative pull test can be performed by hand by pulling silicone with reasonable strength. Nevertheless, this mismatch affects stress distribution during pull-off or peel procedures. Regulatory compliance often dictates the mechanical method selected. Note that while these standards evaluate peel strength, the mechanical action is tensile pull-off, distinguishing it from flexible peel testing defined by ISO 8510-2 which covers 180-degree peel test for flexible-bonded-to-rigid test specimen assembly. These distinctions are vital for the research question, as epoxy coatings typically require tensile pull-off (ASTM D4541), while flexible silicone or urethane membranes may require peel configurations (ASTM D1876). Cross-cut test classification based on percentage of area affected by coating removal provides a visual metric distinct from force measurements [68]. This prohibition underscores the necessity of method-specific benchmarks when comparing epoxy, urethane, and silicone materials. * Pull-off testing applies perpendicular tensile force via a dolly to provide quantitative adhesion strength values in MPa or psi, distinguishing it from qualitative cross-cut methods. * Standardization trends show a progression from qualitative tape tests (ASTM D3359, 1974) to quantitative pull-off methods (ISO 4624, 2023), influencing regulatory compliance for polymer coatings. * Mechanical suitability varies by material, with pull-off tests typical for epoxy resins and rigid substrates, while peel tests are suitable for flexible coatings experiencing prying or bending [25]. * Peel and pull-off data mechanisms differ significantly, preventing direct comparison of strength values across these testing methodologies. ! Coating Adhesion Test Standards and Measurements*. ## ASTM D4541 Protocols and Pull-Off Standardization ASTM D4541 serves as the primary framework for quantifying pull-off strength, distinguishing itself from qualitative alternatives through specific protocol requirements. This method is recognized as the standard test method for pull-off strength of coatings using portable testers [15]. Indeed, the distinction between quantitative and qualitative evaluation is critical when comparing epoxy, urethane, and silicone materials, as performance metrics differ significantly across these chemistries. This divergence influences material selection, particularly for structural applications where precise bond strength data is required. For polymer coatings where tensile integrity is paramount, pull-off protocols offer superior data fidelity. | Pull-Off | ASTM D4541 | Quantitative | Tensile Force |. | Cross-Cut | ASTM D3359 | Qualitative | 0B to 5B Scale [18] |. | Cross-Cut | ISO 2409 | Qualitative | Detachment Evaluation |. This creates a distinction between general coating standards and substrate-specific protocols. While D4541 is broad, D7234 is specialized, suggesting that concrete applications require distinct validation compared to metallic substrates used for epoxy or urethane systems. The existence of multiple standards necessitates careful selection based on the substrate material to ensure regulatory compliance. A significant conflict exists regarding the fundamental nature of the data produced by these tests. This discrepancy implies that while pull-off tests provide quantitative force data, they may reflect cohesive failure within the coating or adhesive rather than pure interfacial adhesion. Therefore, while ASTM D4541 provides numerical values, these values might represent a combination of adhesive and cohesive strengths. For the research question comparing epoxy, urethane, and silicone, this means pull-off data indicates system integrity but may not isolate material-specific interfacial chemistry performance. Material suitability further differentiates these protocols. Thicker polymer systems, common in protective urethane applications, may exceed this constraint, necessitating pull-off methods. However, ASTM D3359 is used as an adhesion standard despite not being designed for conformal coatings [38]. This misapplication risk underscores the need for strict adherence to ASTM D4541 for thicker or non-metallic systems. Specific performance metrics vary by material and substrate combination. These variations highlight that absolute strength values are material-dependent. This suggests that for silicone, pull-off configurations must account for potential contamination effects on the interface. Regulatory compliance extends beyond ASTM to international standards. This contrasts with the 1999 cross-cut specification. The newer 2014 standard aligns with the quantitative trend seen in ASTM D4541, suggesting a shift toward tensile measurement in regulatory frameworks. Compliance with these varied standards requires testing laboratories to maintain equipment capable of both tensile and tape-based evaluations. ASTM D4541 Standard Test Method for Pull-Off Strength requires portable adhesion tester apparatus. The ASTM D4541 Pull-Off Test measures tensile pull-off force required to detach dolly adhesively bonded to coating from substrate. While peel tests address specific stress modes, pull-off tests address normal stress. ASTM D4541 pull-off test is a fundamental standard for measuring adhesive strength on metallic substrates [70]. For epoxy adhesives, toughened epoxy adhesive Bondmaster E3334 was tested with ATBN reactive liquid polymer toughening agent [30]. The performance of such modified epoxies under ASTM D4541 protocols would differ from unmodified systems, necessitating specific calibration. Scoring systems also vary between methods. Method A results assessed on 0A-5A scale where 5A indicates no peeling and 0A indicates complete removal. These qualitative scales contrast with the continuous numerical data from pull-off tests. The FAS-CeO2/PDMS coating achieved cross-cut adhesion classification of level 2 corresponding to 4B rating. This specific data point illustrates how qualitative ratings map to material performance. However, independent laboratories have noted differences of up to 50 percent in tape adhesion variability over time. This variability reinforces the preference for ASTM D4541 in high-stakes applications where precision is required. ASTM-D-4541 standard may be used for portable adhesion tester in laboratory or field environment [71]. This flexibility supports the use of pull-off methods for in-situ verification of epoxy, urethane, and silicone coatings after application. ASTM D4541 standard states scoring around the fixture violates the fundamental test criterion for un. Adherence to these procedural constraints is necessary to validate the quantitative data generated. * ASTM D4541 and ISO 4624 provide quantitative tensile force measurements, whereas ASTM D3359 and ISO 2409 offer qualitative scale-based evaluations. * Conflicting evidence exists on whether pull-off tests measure fundamental adhesion strength or overall system integrity, suggesting results may include cohesive failure components. * Substrate specificity is critical, with ASTM D7234 designated for concrete and ASTM D4541 for general coatings, impacting test selection for polymer systems [10]. * Cross-cut methods are limited to coatings under 5 mils thickness and rigid substrates, restricting their use for thicker urethane or silicone applications. * Variability in tape adhesion testing can reach 50 percent, supporting the use of pull-off protocols for higher precision in regulatory compliance. ## Peel Test Variations Including 90-Degree and T-Peel Configurations Peel test variations offer distinct mechanical insights for flexible polymer coatings compared to pull-off standardization protocols. This distinction is vital for the research question comparing adhesion testing methods across epoxy, urethane, and silicone materials, as the mechanical loading mode significantly influences failure modes. However, the configuration of the peel test dictates the stress distribution at the peel front. Different peel test types include 90-degree peel, 180-degree peel, T-peel, floating roller peel, loop tack strength test, and Double Cantilever Beam test. In contrast, the 180° peel strength test involves bonding two materials, stripping part of the material, and applying force at a 180° angle [31]. For flexible materials bonded to each other, the T-Peel Test is designed to measure the force required to separate two flexible materials that have been bonded. These variations allow analysts to select the geometry that best mimics the service conditions of the polymer coating. **Table 1: Comparison of Peel Test Configurations and Standards**. | Configuration | Primary Standard | Substrate Suitability | Measurement Unit |. | 90-Degree Peel | ASTM D6862 | Semi-rigid or rigid | Force per width |. | 180-Degree Peel | ISO 8510-2 | Flexible-bonded-to-rigid | N/mm or lb/in |. | T-Peel | ASTM D1876 | Thin-gauge metal adherends | Force per width |. | Cross-Cut | ISO 2409 | Thin coatings on rigid substrates [21] | Classification 0B-5B |. This granularity allows for more nuanced performance tracking than the binary pass/fail nature of some tape tests. This disagreement highlights a critical trade-off in adhesion testing: practical bond strength evaluation versus theoretical interfacial energy measurement. The position that tests measure practical bond strength has stronger support across the evidence base, as multiple sources define these methods by their ability to quantify resistance to separation. The pull-off device provides values for the peel stress, which not only allows a quick determination of the adhesion of the coating to the substrate, but also makes it easier to compare the adhesion of several coatings to each other. Testing speed is another variable influencing peel test outcomes. In one study, peel speed was varied from 0.5 to 1.5mm/s to test effects on three coatings [54]. Results showed little dependence on peel speed, but low adhesion strength of silicones was apparent. This shows that while speed is a controlled parameter in standards, it may not be the dominant factor for certain polymer chemistries like silicone. Peel force is applied to an edge of the adherend and substrate and determined from average value. Material suitability varies significantly across testing methods. This makes peel testing more appropriate for flexible polymer coatings such as urethanes and silicones used in roofing or conformal applications, whereas cross-cut methods favor rigid epoxy systems on metal. Standards compliance ensures reproducibility across these variations. Specifically, the existence of multiple standards for similar measurements indicates that regulatory compliance depends on the specific industry sector, such as automotive versus construction. This global proliferation of standards necessitates careful selection based on geographic regulatory requirements. Sample preparation introduces further variables affecting data integrity. However, conflicting evidence suggests a coating adhesion test requires pulling cheesecloth at 180 degree angle at slow steady rate without specifying dimensions. Environmental control is therefore critical when comparing data across different laboratories or field conditions. Roof coating must cure minimum 72 hours before conducting peel adhesion test. This curing requirement is critical for urethane and silicone roof coatings where full chemical crosslinking takes time. Performance scoring differs between quantitative and qualitative methods. Tape test procedures involve applying pressure-sensitive adhesive tape on cross-hatched coating area and removing quickly. This high variability supports the use of peel tests for critical applications requiring precise numerical data. However, paint and tape adhesion tests are useful for screening surface prep and cure on coatings. Thus, tape methods serve a quality control function while peel and pull-off methods serve performance validation functions. Material specific performance also dictates method selection. Adhesive formulations are designed to provide excellent adhesion and high shear and peel strength [72]. Silicone materials exhibited low adhesion strength in peel contexts. This aligns with the known lower surface energy of silicones compared to epoxies and urethanes. Consequently, testing silicone coatings may require more sensitive peel configurations or surface energy measurements as complementary methods. Thickness constraints further limit method applicability. This narrow range excludes many thick polymer linings used in industrial settings. This failure mode requirement ensures that cohesive failure within the coating is distinguished from adhesive failure at the interface. Regulatory frameworks reinforce these methodological distinctions. Peel force peaks occur at 60° and 180° peel angles where more peeling force is borne by tape backing [29]. This mechanical phenomenon shows that 90-degree configurations might offer a more direct measure of interfacial adhesion by minimizing backing deformation effects. This division between field and laboratory methods underscores the trade-off between precision and portability. Cross-cut tape peel test was used to evaluate the adhesion property of polymeric coatings by cutting a lattice pattern of small squares [73]. Surface contaminants and preparation impact all methods. Using a calibrated knife reduced subjectivity found in other adhesion tests. However, Cross-cut tape peel test was used to evaluate the adhesion property of polymeric coatings by cutting a lattice pattern of small squares [73]. Consistency in cut depth is therefore paramount for reproducible cross-cut results. Scrape Test procedure applies adhesive to smooth substrate at uniform thickness then uses rounded stylus [17]. * Peel test configurations (90-degree, 180-degree, T-peel) provide quantitative force per width data (N/mm or lb/in) suitable for flexible polymer coatings, unlike the qualitative 0B-5B classification of cross-cut tests. * Peel speed variations (0.5 to 1.5mm/s) showed little dependence in silicone materials, suggesting chemistry may influence speed sensitivity more than test geometry. * Methodological conflicts exist regarding whether tests measure fundamental adhesion strength or practical bond resistance, with evidence favoring the latter for engineering applications. * Regulatory compliance requires adherence to specific standards such as ASTM D1876 for T-peel or ISO 2409 for cross-cut, depending on substrate type and geographic jurisdiction. ## Peel Testing Standards ASTM D903 and ISO11339 Compliance Peel testing standards such as ASTM D903 and ISO 11339 provide the structural basis for quantifying adhesive strength in flexible coating systems, distinguishing them from qualitative alternatives. ASTM D903-98 specifically governs peel adhesion testing conducted at a 180-degree angle [57]. These standards differ fundamentally from cross-cut and pull-off methods, which serve distinct evaluative purposes within the regulatory landscape. This distinction is critical for regulatory compliance, as quantitative data is often required for safety submissions. To contextualize these methods within the research question regarding epoxy, urethane, and silicone materials, the following table compares the operational parameters of the primary adhesion testing standards. |:--- |:--- |:--- |:--- |:--- |:--- |:--- |. | Peel Test | ASTM D903 / ISO 11339 | 180-degree or T-Peel | Quantitative (Force/Width) | Flexible coatings | N/A | N/mm or lb/in |. | Cross-Cut | ASTM D3359 / ISO 2409 | Lattice Pattern | Qualitative (0B-5B) | Metallic substrates | 250 micrometers | Classification Scale |. | Tape Test | ASTM D3359 Method B | Pressure-Sensitive Tape | Qualitative | Metal or Plastic | 5 mils | 0A-5A Scale |. This calculation yields units such as N/mm or lb/in, allowing for precise comparison across different polymer coatings. This finding shows that while peel testing is applicable to silicones, the absolute strength values are lower compared to epoxy or urethane systems. Despite these controls, conflicts exist regarding specimen preparation dimensions. This discrepancy highlights a lack of uniformity in strip dimensions across different industry applications, potentially affecting comparability between ASTM D903 compliance and specific manufacturer protocols. Regulatory codification dates further illustrate the evolution of these standards. This progression from cross-cut to pull-off in Japanese standards mirrors the global shift towards quantitative data for regulatory compliance. A significant theoretical conflict exists regarding what these tests actually measure. Relying solely on one method obscures the true performance of urethane or epoxy coatings under stress. Market expectations further define compliance thresholds. This specific metric demonstrates how peel testing standards are adapted for commercial warranty requirements beyond laboratory conditions. This limitation is crucial for the research question, as comparing epoxy pull-off data directly with silicone peel data would yield invalid conclusions regarding relative performance. Thus, for polymer coatings on metal roofing or flexible substrates, peel standards like ASTM D903 are more relevant than cross-cut methods intended for rigid substrates. This restricts the utility of cross-cut testing for non-metallic applications common in urethane and silicone coatings. Such qualitative scales lack the precision required for high-performance regulatory submissions where force per width metrics are mandated. Consequently, while cross-cut tests remain common for quality assurance, peel and pull-off methods are preferred for compliance documentation. This requirement underscores that standard compliance is not merely about the test execution but also about preconditioning. ASTM Committee D01 standards requiring tape for adhesion testing are under ballot review. This ongoing review indicates that even established standards like ASTM D3359 are subject to revision based on new performance data. For the comparative analysis of polymer coatings, this implies that reliance on older tape test standards may not reflect current best practices for epoxy or urethane systems. Global harmonization of such standards facilitates the comparison of coating performance across different regulatory regions. This multi-standard approach ensures compatibility with both domestic and international markets. This high variability further supports the preference for peel testing standards like ASTM D903 when quantitative precision is required for the research question. In summary, ASTM D903 and ISO 11339 provide the necessary framework for quantitative peel testing, distinguishing them from the qualitative limitations of cross-cut methods. While conflicts exist regarding specimen dimensions and the fundamental nature of the measurement, the consensus favors quantitative methods for regulatory compliance. In contrast, the evidence shows that for silicone materials, peel strength is lower but measurable, whereas epoxy systems show strong correlation with peel test results. Urethane materials, while less explicitly detailed in the evidence, fall under the general coating standards applicable to metallic and rigid substrates. The divergence in standards codification dates and ongoing ballot reviews indicate a dynamic regulatory environment that prioritizes quantitative data for safety and warranty verification. * ASTM D903-98 and ISO 11339:1990 govern 180-degree peel testing, providing quantitative force/width metrics unlike qualitative cross-cut methods. * Conflicting evidence exists regarding peel strip dimensions (1x6 inches vs 1x12 inches) and whether tests measure fundamental bond strength, requiring careful method selection. !. This is the real shared-state hazard the triage exposes. The conftest.py fix above only resolves COLLECTION errors; it does NOT unify the two namespaces into one module instance. Tests that assert against shared module state need to import via the SAME spelling that the production path uses. The mitigation: I-bug-100 already standardized on `src.polaris_graph.llm.openrouter_client` for the cost-tracking ContextVars. The follow-up issue should also document the canonical convention as `from src.polaris_graph.X import Y` and migrate the 65 affected test files to that spelling.
